<div style="display:inline-block; padding:0.2em 0.4em; line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Irresistible_grace" title="Irresistible grace"><b>Irresistible grace</b></a></div><br />

<div style="display:inline-block; padding:0.2em 0.4em; line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Sola_gratia" title="Sola gratia"><i><b>Sola gratia</b></i></a></div><br />
<div style="display:inline-block; padding:0.2em 0.4em; line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Sacrament" title="Sacrament"><b>Sacraments</b></a></div>
<hr /></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1129693374"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Grace_in_Christianity" title="Template:Grace in Christianity"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Grace_in_Christianity" title="Template talk:Grace in Christianity"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:Grace_in_Christianity" title="Special:EditPage/Template:Grace in Christianity"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Free grace theology</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Christian" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian">Christian</a> <a href="/wiki/Soteriology" title="Soteriology">soteriological</a> view which holds that the only condition of salvation is faith, excluding good works and perseverance, holding to <a href="/wiki/Eternal_security" title="Eternal security">eternal security</a>. Free Grace advocates believe that good works are not the condition to merit (as with <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Catholics</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> to maintain (as with <a href="/wiki/Arminianism" title="Arminianism">Arminians</a>), or to prove (as with most <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinists</a>) salvation, but rather are part of discipleship and the basis for receiving eternal rewards (unlike in <a href="/wiki/Hyper-Grace_theology" title="Hyper-Grace theology">Hyper-Grace</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Free_Grace_Soteriology_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Free_Grace_Soteriology-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Final_Destiny:_The_Future_Reign_of_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Final_Destiny:_The_Future_Reign_of-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Webmaster_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webmaster-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> This soteriological view distinguishes between salvation and discipleship&#160;&#8211;&#32;the call to believe in Christ as Savior and to receive the gift of eternal life, and the call to follow Christ and become an obedient <a href="/wiki/Disciple_(Christianity)" title="Disciple (Christianity)">disciple</a>, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Free grace theologians emphasize the absolute freeness of salvation and the possibility of full assurance that is not grounded upon personal performance.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Norman_Geisler" title="Norman Geisler">Norman Geisler</a> has divided this view into a moderate form and a more radical form. The moderate form being associated with <a href="/wiki/Charles_Caldwell_Ryrie" title="Charles Caldwell Ryrie">Charles Ryrie</a>, and the more strong form with <a href="/wiki/Zane_C._Hodges" title="Zane C. Hodges">Zane Hodges</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:11_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The modern form of Free grace theology has its roots in the soteriology of formulated by many <a href="/wiki/Dispensationalism" title="Dispensationalism">dispensational</a> theologians, this form of soteriology was coined with the title "<i>Free grace</i>" by <a href="/wiki/Zane_C._Hodges" title="Zane C. Hodges">Zane Hodges</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lordship_Salvation_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lordship_Salvation-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_grace_theology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Church">Early Church</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_grace_theology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early Church"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to Ken Wilson, we find <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine</a> mentioning unnamed individuals who held to the view that one is saved by faith alone and that God's future judgement for Christians only consisted of temporal punishment and reward, hell being out of question. Thus, they held that deeds such as repentance and good works were not necessary to enter heaven. Their views were criticized by Augustine.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Jody Dillow quoted <a href="/wiki/Pseudo-Chrysostom" title="Pseudo-Chrysostom">Pseudo-Chrysostom</a> (6th century) as holding to the view held by some Free Grace theologians that the one who does not obey will be in the kingdom but not "reign" with Christ.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_history">Later history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_grace_theology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Later history"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:LewisSperryChafer1929.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/02/LewisSperryChafer1929.jpg/144px-LewisSperryChafer1929.jpg" decoding="async" width="144" height="217" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/02/LewisSperryChafer1929.jpg/216px-LewisSperryChafer1929.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/0/02/LewisSperryChafer1929.jpg 2x" data-file-width="260" data-file-height="392" /></a><figcaption>Lewis Sperry Chafer (1871 – 1952) influenced modern Free Grace theologians.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:23_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:23-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Wayne_Grudem" title="Wayne Grudem">Wayne Grudem</a> has noted that some Free grace advocates teach similar views as <a href="/wiki/Robert_Sandeman_(theologian)" title="Robert Sandeman (theologian)">Robert Sandeman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Free Grace views of the assurance of salvation were also found very commonly among early <a href="/wiki/Dispensationalism" title="Dispensationalism">Dispensationalists</a>, this includes <a href="/wiki/James_Hall_Brookes" title="James Hall Brookes">James Hall Brookes</a> and <a href="/wiki/C._I._Scofield" title="C. I. Scofield">C. I. Scofield</a>, who argued for every believer's right for absolute assurance of salvation, however many of them still held to a soft form of the <a href="/wiki/Perseverance_of_the_saints" title="Perseverance of the saints">perseverance of the saints</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The modern movement can find much of its roots in the theological views articulated by <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Sperry_Chafer" title="Lewis Sperry Chafer">Lewis Sperry Chafer</a> (February 27, 1871 – August 22, 1952), who published the book "He That Is Spiritual" in which he articulated many Free Grace viewpoints. This caused a smaller scale controversy in his day, when B. B. Warfield took issue with Chafer's doctrine. His views were a major influence upon modern proponents of Free Grace theology.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:23_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:23-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Slightly prior to the <a href="/wiki/Lordship_salvation_controversy" title="Lordship salvation controversy">Lordship salvation controversy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Everett_F._Harrison" title="Everett F. Harrison">Everett Harrison</a> opposed the view that one must make Christ "Lord of your life" and make a commitment to follow Jesus in order to be justified. Everett Harrison had a debate with John Stott on the issue in 1959, mirroring the Lordship salvation controversy.<sup id="cite_ref-Ministries_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ministries-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:ZaneHodges.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0e/ZaneHodges.JPG" decoding="async" width="125" height="179" class="mw-file-element" data-file-width="125" data-file-height="179" /></a><figcaption>Zane C. Hodges</figcaption></figure>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Lordship_salvation_controversy" title="Lordship salvation controversy">Lordship salvation controversy</a> involved those holding to Free Grace theology. The debate was centered around the question on whether accepting Jesus Christ as saviour necessarily implies one must make a concrete commitment in life toward the Christ such as following a certain behaviour or <a href="/wiki/Moral_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Moral system">moral system</a>. The debate surfaced when <a href="/wiki/John_MacArthur_(American_pastor)" title="John MacArthur (American pastor)">John McArthur's</a> book "The Gospel According to Jesus" generated a strong response from those holding to Free Grace theology. The first to respond against the views of John McArthur was <a href="/wiki/Charles_Caldwell_Ryrie" title="Charles Caldwell Ryrie">Charles Ryrie</a>, who wrote the book "So Great Salvation" where he articulated Free Grace theology. This was followed by <a href="/wiki/Zane_C._Hodges" title="Zane C. Hodges">Zane Hodges</a> publishing his books against the Lordship salvation view. The debate was reignited in the 21st century, when <a href="/wiki/Wayne_Grudem" title="Wayne Grudem">Wayne Grudem</a> wrote against Free Grace theology, causing much new literature to be written on the topic.<sup id="cite_ref-faithalone.org_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faithalone.org-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ministries_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ministries-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:23_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:23-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Around the 1990s, <a href="/wiki/Zane_C._Hodges" title="Zane C. Hodges">Zane Hodges</a> began to articulate what has been called the "crossless gospel" (although often seen as a derogatory term) which is the belief that one must only believe in Jesus' promise of eternal life to be saved, knowledge of the substitutionary atonement being unnecessary for salvation. This evolved into the crossless gospel controversy in 2005, when the Grace Evangelical society officially declared its stance on faith to include only Jesus' promise of eternal life to the one who believes, causing many members to leave the society. Other Free Grace theologians such as David R. Anderson, Joseph Dillow, Charlie Bing and Charles Ryrie hold that one must believe in the person and work of Christ to be saved, disagreeing with the view of the Grace Evangelical society.<sup id="cite_ref-:10_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:6_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:8_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:23_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:23-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:9_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>More modern prominent proponents, academicians, and theologians include:
</p>

<li><a href="/wiki/M._R._DeHaan" title="M. R. DeHaan">M.R. DeHaan</a><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup></li></ul></div>
</div>
<p>Its prominent present-day expressions are Grace School of Theology,<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Grace_Evangelical_Society" title="Grace Evangelical Society">Grace Evangelical Society</a> and the Free Grace Alliance.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Free Grace theology has been mainly taught by individuals among: <a href="/wiki/Southern_Baptist_Convention" title="Southern Baptist Convention">Southern Baptists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Independent_Baptist" title="Independent Baptist">Independent Baptists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plymouth_Brethren" title="Plymouth Brethren">Plymouth Brethren</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calvary_Chapel_Association" title="Calvary Chapel Association">Calvary Chapel churches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nondenominational_Christianity" title="Nondenominational Christianity">non-Denominational churches</a>, Churches affiliated with Florida Bible College, <a href="/wiki/Bible_church" title="Bible church">Bible churches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Local_churches_(affiliation)" title="Local churches (affiliation)">Local churches</a> influenced by <a href="/wiki/Watchman_Nee" title="Watchman Nee">Watchman Nee</a>, Doctrinal Churches influenced by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Thieme" title="Robert Thieme">R. B. Thieme</a> and other Independent churches.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dallas_Theological_Seminary">Dallas Theological Seminary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_grace_theology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Dallas Theological Seminary"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Many modern proponents of free grace theology studied and taught at the <a href="/wiki/Dallas_Theological_Seminary" title="Dallas Theological Seminary">Dallas Theological Seminary</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Charles_Caldwell_Ryrie" title="Charles Caldwell Ryrie">Charles Caldwell Ryrie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zane_C._Hodges" title="Zane C. Hodges">Zane C. Hodges</a>, and Dave Anderson, though the seminary itself does not hold to free grace. A number of free grace churches are pastored by graduates of Dallas Theological Seminary.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> A number of opponents of free grace also graduated from Dallas Theological Seminary, including Darrel Bock<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> and Daniel Wallace.
</p><p>Dallas Theological Seminary was more influenced by Free Grace theology during the 20th century, with multiple faculty members holding Free Grace soteriological views.  Despite the influence of Free Grace theology at Dallas Theological Seminary, its popularity has declined in the Dallas Theological Seminary.<sup id="cite_ref-:23_16-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:23-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Grace_School_of_Theology">Grace School of Theology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_grace_theology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Grace School of Theology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Dave Anderson, former student and professor at <a href="/wiki/Dallas_Theological_Seminary" title="Dallas Theological Seminary">Dallas Theological Seminary</a>, established Grace School of Theology (originally Houston Theological Seminary)<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> in 2001. Grace School of Theology "is committed to Christian scholarly endeavor in the free grace tradition."<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The school's vision is "To develop spiritual leaders in every nation who can teach others about the love of Christ, a love that cannot be earned and cannot be lost."<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The school is accredited by TRACS, ATS, and the ECFA<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> with 14 teaching sites in the United States and internationally.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Eight of the 36 faculty members trained at <a href="/wiki/Dallas_Theological_Seminary" title="Dallas Theological Seminary">Dallas Theological Seminary</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Grace School of Theology promotes the Free Grace position through its classes (with over 600 students internationally) and also through Grace Theology Press, which has published many resources related to Free Grace theology.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Free_Grace_Alliance">Free Grace Alliance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_grace_theology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Free Grace Alliance"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Free Grace Alliance formed in November 2004 with an emphasis on international missions.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Although the new organization was officially formed for a "different reason",<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> the Free Grace Alliance split from the Grace Evangelical Society in 2005 when most of the prominent leaders (including the chairman of the board) within <a href="/wiki/Grace_Evangelical_Society" title="Grace Evangelical Society">GES</a> rejected the change in the content of saving faith being taught by <a href="/wiki/Zane_C._Hodges" title="Zane C. Hodges">Zane C. Hodges</a> and the Grace Evangelical Society changed its doctrinal statement regarding the content of saving faith.<sup id="cite_ref-Reiher_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reiher-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> A non-association statement was made in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The Free Grace Alliance holds annual conferences, and numerous local churches and Christian ministries are associated with the alliance as members or affiliates.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Grace_Evangelical_Society">Grace Evangelical Society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_grace_theology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Grace Evangelical Society"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Founded in 1986 by Robert Wilkin, the <a href="/wiki/Grace_Evangelical_Society" title="Grace Evangelical Society">Grace Evangelical Society</a> focuses on publishing, podcasts, and conferences. The Grace Evangelical Society was a focal point for the mainstream Free Grace movement until 2005, when it officially altered its beliefs statement to say that eternal life and eternal security are synonymous<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> and that belief in eternal security provided by Jesus is the sole requirement for salvation.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Zane_C._Hodges" title="Zane C. Hodges">Zane C. Hodges</a>, a prominent Free Grace theologian, was a core theologian of the group until his death in 2008. In his later years, Zane Hodges controversially argued that the inclusion of Jesus' promise of eternal salvation was a necessity for proper evangelization.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> He viewed the sole condition of eternal salvation as believing in Jesus' promise of eternal life, and Grace Evangelical Society began to promote this view increasingly.<sup id="cite_ref-Reiher_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reiher-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> In this view, a person could believe that Jesus is God and Savior who died and rose again, without believing in him for eternal salvation (faith in eternal security), and could therefore still be damned. A person could also become a Christian by believing in someone named Jesus for eternal security, while rejecting that he is God and Savior from sin by his death and resurrection.<sup id="cite_ref-Wilson_2020_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson_2020-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> According to the society's website salvation comes from "faith alone in the Lord Jesus Christ, who died a substitutionary death on the cross for man’s sin and rose bodily from the dead" <sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> However, society proponents deny that a person must believe in the substitutionary death for sin and a bodily resurrection of Jesus to be a Christian.<sup id="cite_ref-Wilson_2020_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson_2020-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The change in the Grace Evangelical Society's official doctrinal statement caused many members (including the chairman of the board) and the majority of academic members to leave the society in 2005–2006. Almost all free grace academic theologians rejected the new statement, arguing that eternal life and eternal security are not the same thing.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> They also objected that this view would by consequence damn all Christians from the time of 100 A.D. until the 1500s, since there is no evidence that anyone believed in eternal security.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> John Niemelä of the Grace Evangelical Society responded that the promise of eternal life was present during that time through the regular reading of the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">Gospel of John</a> in the lectionaries.<sup id="cite_ref-Niemelä_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Niemelä-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> However, Wilson responded that Niemelä's contention was based on an informal logical fallacy and a heresy.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Grace Evangelical Society additionally runs an unaccredited seminary, called the Grace Evangelical Society Seminary.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_expressions_of_Free_Grace_theology">Other expressions of Free Grace theology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_grace_theology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Other expressions of Free Grace theology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some other smaller non-accredited seminaries such as the Chafer Theological Seminary and Grace Biblical Seminary promote Free Grace theology, and train pastors in the Free Grace view.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> The view has also been majorly promoted by the Florida Bible College which trained hundreds of Free Grace pastors. The Florida Bible College at its peak had around 1500 students, though it shut down in the late nineties, being restarted in 2013 by Ralph "Yankee" Arnold.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Free Grace International is a Free Grace organization, worked on by Larry C Kitchen, Lucas Kitchen and Shawn Lazar (who also worked in GES).<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> The organization attempts to promote Free grace theology worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Beliefs">Beliefs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_grace_theology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Beliefs"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Core_beliefs_table">Core beliefs table</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_grace_theology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Core beliefs table"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Core beliefs common to Free Grace theology historically include:
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<tbody><tr>
<th>Belief</th>
<th>Explanation
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Faith alone</td>
<td>God declares a person righteous by faith in Christ (imputed righteousness) regardless of works accompanying faith either before or after. John 3:14–17 compares believing in Jesus to the Israelites looking upon the <a href="/wiki/Nehushtan" title="Nehushtan">bronze serpent</a> in the wilderness for healing from deadly venom (Numbers 21).<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Relationship differs from intimacy</td>
<td>A permanent relationship with God as Father and the believer as a child begins by faith alone. When someone believes, there is a "<a href="/wiki/Born_again" title="Born again">new birth</a>" and this spiritual birth cannot be undone. However, the familial relationship does not guarantee fellowship; intimacy with God requires obedience.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Justification differs from sanctification</td>
<td>Justification before God is a free unconditional gift by faith alone but <a href="/wiki/Sanctification" class="mw-redirect" title="Sanctification">sanctification</a> requires obedience to God. Sanctification of all Christians is not guaranteed. Only final <a href="/wiki/Glorification" title="Glorification">glorification</a> of all Christians to a sinless state is guaranteed (Romans 8:30; Philippians 2:12).<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Eternal security</td>
<td>Once a person has believed in Jesus Christ as God and Savior that person spends eternity with God regardless of subsequent behavior. God's <a href="/wiki/Perseverance_of_the_saints#Free_Grace_doctrine" title="Perseverance of the saints">eternal acceptance</a> is unconditionally given. Belonging to God's family is a permanent and irrevocable gift (Romans 11:29).<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Assurance of salvation</td>
<td>Confidence of spending eternity with God is possible for every Christian since God justifies through faith alone and provides eternal security.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Free_Grace_Soteriology_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Free_Grace_Soteriology-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Rewards and discipline</td>
<td>All Christians will undergo judgment by Christ based upon their works and degree of conformity to Christ's character (or lack thereof). This is called the judgment seat or Bema Seat of Christ, where Christians are rewarded based on obedience to God through faith.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> This judgment does not concern heaven or hell but rewards (payment for service) or temporary punishment. God's familial acceptance of his children is unconditionally given. However, God's payments of eternal honor, riches, and positions of authority are only given for children who obediently served God.  Good parents discipline their children and will not approve behavior that is detrimental. Neither will God approve sinful behavior that leads to destructive consequences (Hebrews 12:5–11).<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soteriology">Soteriology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_grace_theology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Soteriology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Free Grace theology is distinguished by holding a strong version of the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Sola_fide" title="Sola fide">faith alone</a>. Free Grace theology holds that things such as turning from sin, baptism or perseverance in the faith are not necessary for salvation, but instead hold that these things are necessary for eternal rewards.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Free Grace writers generally agree that good works do not play a role in meriting, maintaining, or proving eternal life. In other words, Jesus <i>graciously</i> provides eternal salvation as a free gift to those who believe in Him.<sup id="cite_ref-Doct_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doct-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Free Grace theologians universally hold to <a href="/wiki/Eternal_security" title="Eternal security">eternal security</a>, however they deny that every believer will necessarily persevere.<sup id="cite_ref-:23_16-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:23-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Thus, Free Grace theologians hold that anyone who believes in Jesus Christ will go to heaven regardless of any future actions—including future sin, unbelief, or apostasy—though Christians who sin or abandon the faith will face God's discipline. For example, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Thieme" title="Robert Thieme">Robert Thieme</a> states <i>“Although the believer can never lose his eternal life, he can be in danger of destroying his spiritual life and losing all the blessings that “God has prepared for those who love him”.</i><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Free Grace theology is distinguished from so called "<a href="/wiki/Hyper-Grace_theology" title="Hyper-Grace theology">Hyper-Grace</a>" views taught by a few Charismatic teachers, by arguing that a believer may experience temporal judgement for sin.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Free Grace theology is distinguished by the treatment of the words "salvation" and "save" in the bible. Free Grace theologians argue that there are many ways believers can experience "salvation", not necessarily referring to salvation from hell. This view is seen from verses such as Acts 27:34 where the Greek word <i>soteria</i> (typically translated as "salvation") is translated “health” or "strength" because food will assist their deliverance from physical death. Spiritually, salvation has been seen as referring to deliverance from the eternal penalty of sin (<a href="/wiki/Justification_(theology)" title="Justification (theology)">justification</a>), the current power of sin over the Christian (<a href="/wiki/Sanctification" class="mw-redirect" title="Sanctification">sanctification</a>), the removal of any possibility to sin (<a href="/wiki/Glorification" title="Glorification">glorification</a>), and being restored to <a href="/wiki/Stewardship_(theology)" title="Stewardship (theology)">stewardship</a> over the world as God intended for humankind at creation (restoration to rule).<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are some differences among Free Grace theologians on the issue of fruit in a Christian life. More moderate Free Grace theologians still affirm that faith will necessarily lead into good works, although it may not be outwardly evident or last to the end of one's life. However, those who hold to a more strong form of Free Grace theology deny that every Christian will bear fruit in their life.<sup id="cite_ref-:11_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Repentance">Repentance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_grace_theology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Repentance"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Free grace theology approaches the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Repentance" title="Repentance">repentance</a> in a different way than most other Christian traditions. Free Grace theologians have generally held one of three views on repentance:<sup id="cite_ref-:23_16-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:23-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A major number of Free Grace theologians, including: <a href="/wiki/Harry_A._Ironside" title="Harry A. Ironside">Harry A. Ironside</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Sperry_Chafer" title="Lewis Sperry Chafer">Lewis Sperry Chafer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Caldwell_Ryrie" title="Charles Caldwell Ryrie">Charles Ryrie</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Walvoord" title="John Walvoord">Walvoord</a>, <a href="/wiki/J._Dwight_Pentecost" title="J. Dwight Pentecost">Pentecost</a>, Charlie Bing and others have taught that repentance (<i>metanoia</i>) should be treated as a change of mind not as a turning from sin or sorrow for sin. Thus, in this view repentance is viewed as a synonym for faith.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:23_16-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:23-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A second view was suggested by <a href="/wiki/Zane_C._Hodges" title="Zane C. Hodges">Zane C. Hodges</a>, David Anderson and Robert Wilkin (although initially holding to the view of Ryrie and Chafer),<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> in which repentance is defined as turning from one's sins, but repentance is not a requirement for eternal life, only faith in Christ. Zane Hodges presented this view in his book "Harmony with God", where he argued that repentance is not a condition of salvation, but is a condition of fellowship with God and sanctification. However, repentance may be preached to unbelievers, in which case it makes one more disposed to faith in Christ. In this view, passages such as <i>Luke 13:3</i> are viewed temporarily and corporately, Zane Hodges argued that Jesus is warning the nation of Israel of the destruction of Judea by the Romans.<sup id="cite_ref-:23_16-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:23-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Joseph Dillow taught instead that repentance refers to remorse or regret for sin, in his view being a necessary pre-condition of faith. However, Dillow rejected the view that repentance should be viewed as commitment to Christ.<sup id="cite_ref-:23_16-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:23-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judgement_seat_of_Christ">Judgement seat of Christ</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_grace_theology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Judgement seat of Christ"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Free Grace theologians put a heavy emphasis on the doctrine of eternal rewards which are determined in the judgement seat of Christ. In the Free Grace system, passages which seem to connect justification with good works, are instead viewed as referencing eternal rewards, and not eternal salvation. The view that individuals will have differing degrees of reward depending upon their service is based on an interpretation of Paul's words in the first epistle to the Corinthians, in which he references being saved "through fire". Free Grace theologians have taken this to mean that those who have not served Christ will be saved, though forfeiting eternal rewards.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:23_16-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:23-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:33_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:33-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> This view of eternal rewards has been influenced by the writings of <a href="/wiki/C._I._Scofield" title="C. I. Scofield">Scofield</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Jody Dillow divided eternal rewards into three categories (a) rewards of enhanced intimacy (b) rewards of honor and (c) rewards of service.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Divine_discipline">Divine discipline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_grace_theology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Divine discipline"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Free Grace theologians hold that unrighteous believers will experience severe divine discipline. Free Grace theologians often interpret the warnings in the book of Hebrews, such as those at the 10th and 6th chapter to be warnings of severe divine discipline for apostasy.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Although some such as Norman Geisler understood these warnings as pertaining to eternal rewards.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epistles_of_James_and_John">Epistles of James and John</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_grace_theology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Epistles of James and John"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are some differences among Free Grace theologians on the role of good works as results of salvation due to their respective interpretations of the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_James" title="Epistle of James">Epistle of James</a>. Free Grace theologians such as Bob Wilkin, <a href="/wiki/Zane_C._Hodges" title="Zane C. Hodges">Zane Hodges</a> Joseph Dillow among others hold that the one who possesses "dead faith" as mentioned <i>James 2:17</i> is not a false convert<i>,</i> in this view the word "dead" is referring to a faith that is not profitable in this life nor in the judgement seat of Christ, but does not imply false conversion. Thus, when the epistle of James says "can that faith save him", it is either understood as salvation from temporal consequences of sin (Zane Hodges), salvation from a loss of reward (Charlie Bing), both (Joseph Dillow) or as the physical salvation of the poor person described in the chapter (R. T. Kendall).<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> These Free Grace theologians have argued that James contextually thus does not speak of eternal salvation.<sup id="cite_ref-:23_16-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:23-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Webmaster_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webmaster-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>  Kenneth Wilson argued that <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine</a> erred in his view of James 2 that has led to the view that the "false faith of demons" lacks works while "true faith" must always produce good works.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In contrast, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Caldwell_Ryrie" title="Charles Caldwell Ryrie">Charles Ryrie</a>, though being a Free grace theologian, believed that faith naturally leads into good works, interpreting James to refer to eternal salvation. Ryrie still held in opposition to Lordship salvation that the believer may not always have fruit nor the fruit be necessarily outwardly evident. Ryrie added that believers will have fruit "somehow, somewhere", but agreed that the category of "carnal Christian" is possible. Ryrie criticized the Lordship salvationist view of good works, as making people into "fruit inspectors".<sup id="cite_ref-:4_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Reformed theologians</a> have often taken the <a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_of_John" title="First Epistle of John">first epistle of John</a> to be written as a test to be able to know if you are justified. However, in the Free Grace view the epistle is viewed as being a test on if the person is in fellowship with God. Thus Free Grace theologians interpret words such as "know" in 1 John to refer to intimacy, instead of as salvation.<sup id="cite_ref-:23_16-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:23-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reigning_with_Christ">Reigning with Christ</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_grace_theology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Reigning with Christ"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The issue of the future millennial reign with Christ has caused controversy among Free Grace theologians, as 2 Timothy 2:12-13 (NIV) reads: <i>"If we died with him, we will also live with him; if we endure, we will also reign with him. If we disown him, he will also disown us;  if we are faithless, he remains faithful, for he cannot disown himself."</i>. Free Grace theologians have understood this passage in multiple different ways. Joseph Dillow among others argued that only the faithful Christians who "overcome" are going to reign with Christ, though the unfaithful will still get into the kingdom, they will not reign with Christ. Other Free Grace advocates believe that this verse does not question if a Christian will reign with Christ as it is a privilege of all Christians, but that a Christian may forfeit their quality of reigning with Christ.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> This issue is connected to the doctrine of the "outer darkness", Joseph Dillow and others such as <a href="/wiki/Charles_Stanley" title="Charles Stanley">Charles Stanley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zane_C._Hodges" title="Zane C. Hodges">Zane Hodges</a> and Ken Wilson have argued that the "outer darkness" as referenced in <i>Matthew 22:13</i> and 2 other passages, is not speaking of hell but of an exclusion from reigning millennium. However, this view of the outer darkness is not taught by all in the Free Grace movement.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:23_16-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:23-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:33_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:33-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> This view has been criticized as teaching a "Protestant purgatory" by its critics, although its advocates have avoided speaking of "Protestant purgatory", denying that it is a place of torment or a necessary step of purification.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rokser_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rokser-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Watchman_Nee" title="Watchman Nee">Watchman Nee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Govett" title="Robert Govett">Robert Govett</a>, D.M Pember and D.M Panton proposed that only righteous believers will enter the millennium. In their view, the believers who lived a carnal lifestyle will have to spend 1000 years in "outer darkness", being a literal though temporal place of torment.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_120-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dispensationalism">Dispensationalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_grace_theology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Dispensationalism"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Modern Free Grace theology is typically, but not necessarily, <a href="/wiki/Dispensationalist_theology" class="mw-redirect" title="Dispensationalist theology">dispensational</a> in its assumptions regarding the philosophy of history and in terms of its networks and affiliations. Some Free Grace theologians have attempted to suggest that Free Grace theology is a natural consequence of dispensationalism.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:3_120-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Although a minority, there are a few non-dispensational individuals who do hold to Free Grace views of soteriology.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Dave Anderson, Free Grace is more connected to the idea of <a href="/wiki/Premillennialism" title="Premillennialism">premillennialism</a> rather than dispensationalism by itself.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Assurance">Assurance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_grace_theology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Assurance"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One of the unique aspects of free grace theology is its position on <a href="/wiki/Assurance_(theology)" title="Assurance (theology)">assurance</a>. All free grace advocates agree that assurance of spending eternity with God is based on the promise of scripture through faith alone in Jesus Christ, and not one's works or subsequent progression in sanctification. This view strongly distinguishes the gift of <a href="/wiki/Eternal_life_(Christianity)" title="Eternal life (Christianity)">eternal life</a> (accompanying <a href="/wiki/Justification_(theology)" title="Justification (theology)">justification</a> by faith) from <a href="/wiki/Disciple_(Christianity)" title="Disciple (Christianity)">discipleship</a> (obedience). Free Grace teaches that a person does not need to promise disciplined behavior or good works in exchange for God's eternal salvation; thus, one cannot lose his or her salvation through sinning and potential failure, and that assurance is based on the Bible, not introspection into one's works. God declares persons righteous through Christ's perfection. Whatever little progress humans make towards perfection is infinitesimal compared to Christ's perfection. Thus, comparing one's progress towards perfection with another person's progress is viewed as unwise (2 Cor 10:12). Assurance is based on Christ's perfection given freely to believers (imputed righteousness) and not based on progressive steps of holiness. <a href="/wiki/Dallas_Theological_Seminary" title="Dallas Theological Seminary">Dallas Theological Seminary</a> sums up the general consensus of free grace theology in Article XI of its doctrinal statement, in reference to assurance:<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>We believe it is the privilege, not only of some, but of all who are born again by the Spirit through faith in Christ as revealed in the Scriptures, to be assured of their salvation from the very day they take Him to be their Savior and that this assurance is not founded upon any fancied discovery of their own worthiness or fitness, but wholly upon the testimony of God in His written Word, exciting within His children filial love, gratitude, and obedience (Luke 10:20; 22:32; 2 Cor. 5:1, 6–8; 2 Tim. 1:12; Heb. 10:22; 1 John 5:13).</dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Content_of_faith">Content of faith</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_grace_theology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Content of faith"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is some controversy on the object of faith among Free Grace theologians. <a href="/wiki/Zane_C._Hodges" title="Zane C. Hodges">Zane Hodges</a> in his later life and the <a href="/wiki/Grace_Evangelical_Society" title="Grace Evangelical Society">Grace Evangelical society</a> have held that faith is assent in the promise of eternal life. The Grace Evangelical Society teaches that knowledge of the deity, atonement and resurrection of Christ is not necessary to be saved, however they are seen as necessary for sanctification.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:23_16-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:23-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:6_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> This view is not shared by all of those who hold to Free Grace theology. Theologians such as Charles Ryrie, Charlie Bing and Jody Dillow view the object of faith as the person and work of Jesus Christ.<sup id="cite_ref-:23_16-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:23-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:4_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:6_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> A smaller scale disagreement exists on if the burial of Christ is necessary for salvation.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Free Grace theologians generally hold that the "quality of faith" does not matter in salvation, but only the object of faith, as Charlie Bing says: "<i>To emphasize the quality of one’s faith necessarily means that the object of faith is de-emphasized".<sup id="cite_ref-:10_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup></i>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discipleship">Discipleship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_grace_theology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Discipleship"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Free Grace theologians distinguish between discipleship and salvation. Free Grace theologians hold that discipleship is a condition of an enhanced experience of life (eternal rewards), however it is not necessary for salvation. Discipleship is neither viewed as an inevitable result of salvation, as Free Grace theology allows for a true Christian to not respond to the call of discipleship. Some Free Grace theologians such as Joseph Dillow, Charlie Bing and Zane Hodges among others even wish to distinguish between Christians who are "overcomers" and those who are not, this view is based on an interpretation of the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">Book of Revelation</a>, referencing "those who overcome". In this view, overcoming is a basis of eternal rewards. However, unlike the distinction between discipleship and salvation, the distinction between "overcomers" and those who do not overcome is not held by all Free Grace theologians, citing 1 John 5:4, which states "For whatever is born of God overcomes the world", though those holding to the distinction argue that the term "overcome" is used differently in Revelation and the <a href="/wiki/Johannine_epistles" title="Johannine epistles">epistle of John</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:23_16-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:23-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hodges_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hodges-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sanctification">Sanctification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_grace_theology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Sanctification"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Free Grace theology holds to a synergistic view of sanctification. Free Grace theology holds that though sanctification is God's work, it is not automatic nor is it passive. If the believer chooses not to cooperate with God's grace, then he will not be sanctified.<sup id="cite_ref-:23_16-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:23-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atonement">Atonement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_grace_theology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Atonement"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Free Grace theologians hold to <a href="/wiki/Unlimited_atonement" title="Unlimited atonement">unlimited atonement</a> and to <a href="/wiki/Penal_substitution" title="Penal substitution">penal substitutionary atonement</a>. Free Grace theology also distinguishes between two kinds of forgiveness, them being positional forgiveness and familial forgiveness. Free Grace theologians hold that positional forgiveness is received through faith alone, while familial forgiveness through confession. Familial forgiveness is not viewed as the basis of salvation but of fellowship and intimacy with God.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:3_120-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election">Election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_grace_theology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Election"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are many views of election within Free Frace theology, with most holding to a form of <a href="/wiki/Conditional_election" title="Conditional election">conditional election</a> and libertarian free will, although some held to a moderate form of Calvinism.<sup id="cite_ref-:23_16-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:23-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Charlie Bing listed the following views as being taught by individuals within Free Grace theology:<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>A moderate form of the <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinistic</a> view of election, this has been taught by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Caldwell_Ryrie" title="Charles Caldwell Ryrie">Charles Ryrie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Sperry_Chafer" title="Lewis Sperry Chafer">Lewis Sperry Chafer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Election grounded upon God's foreknowledge, which is the <a href="/wiki/Arminianism" title="Arminianism">Arminian</a> view of election. Although those Free Grace theologians who hold to this view reject other tenents of Arminianism.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Corporate_election" title="Corporate election">Corporate election</a></li>
<li>Conditional election grounded upon God's middle-knowledge which is <a href="/wiki/Molinism" title="Molinism">Molinism</a>, this has been taught by the Free Grace author John Correia.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Qualitive election which is the view that God's election is always unto service, this has been taught by Shawn Lazar along with the <a href="/wiki/Grace_Evangelical_Society" title="Grace Evangelical Society">Grace Evangelical Society</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_to_the_five_points_of_Reformed_theology">Comparison to the five points of Reformed theology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_grace_theology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Comparison to the five points of Reformed theology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Calvinism#Five_points_of_Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism §&#160;Five points of Calvinism</a></div>
<p>Free Grace contrasts with the teachings of Reformed theology, which are often characterized by the acrostic “TULIP”.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<tbody><tr>
<th>Calvinism</th>
<th>Free grace
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td style="width: 50%;">Total depravity: Humans are not capable of having faith in God because they are totally depraved (total inability).<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup></td>
<td>God gave men the ability to choose, and they are capable of choosing to believe God and believe in Christ (without a divine infusion of faith).<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Unconditional election: Men are not capable of coming to faith on their own (God must infuse faith). God simply chooses to bring some to Himself independently of a choice on the part of the elected person.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup></td>
<td>God desires that all persons should come to faith in Him, and election is according to God's foreknowledge, not only of faith but of all events (1 Peter 1:1-2). (However, a minority of Free Grace theologians have proposed unconditional election, for example <a href="/wiki/Charles_Caldwell_Ryrie" title="Charles Caldwell Ryrie">Charles Ryrie</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-:4_128-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_144-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Limited atonement: Since God only elects some and not others, Christ's death on the cross only applies to the elect. Jesus therefore did not die for the entire world.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup></td>
<td>Jesus died for everyone, but is only effective for those who believe in Christ.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Irresistible grace: Man is totally depraved, God must impose His grace upon the elect in such a way that they are compelled to believe.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup></td>
<td>God's grace can be and is resisted by humans, but is also embraced by humans without divine coercion.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Perseverance of the saints: The only way to know if you have received irresistible grace resulting in saving faith is to see whether you continuously grow in obedience and good works. Obedience and good works are inevitable. Since they view faith as God's gift then faith must be perfect and ultimately produce perfect people.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup></td>
<td>The Christian is eternally secure through God's grace whether or not he/she dies in "state of grace" by persevering in good works. Perseverance in faith is the believer's choice and the means by which believers can achieve maximum joy and fulfillment, both in this life as well as in eternity.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table>

Xulon Press. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-60957-715-5" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-60957-715-5"><bdi>978-1-60957-715-5</bdi></a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Free+Grace+Soteriology&amp;rft.pub=Xulon+Press&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-60957-715-5&amp;rft.aulast=Anderson&amp;rft.aufirst=Dave&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DIK6rBuZcReYC%26dq%3Dfree%2Bgrace%2Btheology%26pg%3DPR9&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFree+grace+theology" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-:11-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-:11_9-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-:11_9-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite id="CITEREFGeisler2021" class="citation book cs1">Geisler, Norman (October 2021). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=bBWkzgEACAAJ"><i>Systematic Theology: In One Volume</i></a>. Bastion Books. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-7376546-0-5" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-7376546-0-5"><bdi>978-1-7376546-0-5</bdi></a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Systematic+Theology%3A+In+One+Volume&amp;rft.pub=Bastion+Books&amp;rft.date=2021-10&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-7376546-0-5&amp;rft.aulast=Geisler&amp;rft.aufirst=Norman&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DbBWkzgEACAAJ&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFree+grace+theology" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Lordship_Salvation-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Lordship_Salvation_10-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Lordship_Salvation_10-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Lordship_Salvation_10-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite class="citation web cs1"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.ligonier.org/guides/lordship-salvation">"Lordship Salvation"</a>. <i>Ligonier Ministries</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2023-12-03</span></span>. <q>During the final two decades of the twentieth century, certain dispensational theologians began to propagate the idea that one could be in a state of salvation and lack entirely the fruit of repentance from sin and obedience to Christ. Their particular form of soteriology came to be known as free grace–a title coined by Zane Hodges. Some of the other more well-known adherents of the Free Grace movement were Louis Sperry Chafer, Miles Stanford, and Norman Geisler. Hodges became a particularly well-known proponent of the Free Grace theology because of his 1981 book The Gospel under Siege.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Ligonier+Ministries&amp;rft.atitle=Lordship+Salvation&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.ligonier.org%2Fguides%2Flordship-salvation&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFree+grace+theology" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite class="citation web cs1"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://gracetheology.org/bookstore/defense-free-grace-theology/">"A Defense of Free Grace Theology"</a>. <i>Grace Theology Press</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2023-09-03</span></span>. <q>There were Christians in good standing with the church c.AD 400 who held the doctrine that a person received salvation by faith alone without repentance or good works. Much to Augustine's ire, baptism was practiced immediately if one of them believed in Christ, without first entering prolonged education in Christian faith and morals as a catechumen. For those early Christians, God's future judgment consisted only of payment (reward) or punishment (temporary) for how those Christians lived their lives before God—heaven or hell was not in question.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Grace+Theology+Press&amp;rft.atitle=A+Defense+of+Free+Grace+Theology&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fgracetheology.org%2Fbookstore%2Fdefense-free-grace-theology%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFree+grace+theology" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite class="citation web cs1"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.newadvent.org/fathers/120121.htm">"CHURCH FATHERS: City of God, Book XXI (St. Augustine)"</a>. <i>www.newadvent.org</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2023-12-01</span></span>. <q>But, say they, the Catholic Christians have Christ for a foundation, and they have not fallen away from union with Him, no matter how depraved a life they have built on this foundation, as wood, hay, stubble; and accordingly the well-directed faith by which Christ is their foundation will suffice to deliver them some time from the continuance of that fire, though it be with loss, since those things they have built on it shall be burned.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=www.newadvent.org&amp;rft.atitle=CHURCH+FATHERS%3A+City+of+God%2C+Book+XXI+%28St.+Augustine%29&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.newadvent.org%2Ffathers%2F120121.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFree+grace+theology" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite class="citation web cs1"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://gracetheology.org/bookstore/final-destiny-the-future-reign-of-the-servant-kings/">"Final Destiny: The Future Reign of the Servant Kings"</a>. <i>Grace Theology Press</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2023-06-08</span></span>. <q>Pseudo-Chrysostom (5th or 6th century AD) that proposed a similar interpretation of Matthew 5:19-20: But seeing that to break the least commandments and not to keep them are one and the same, why does He say above of him that breaks the commandments, that he shall be the least in the kingdom of heaven, and here of him who keeps them not, that he shall not enter into the kingdom of heaven? … For a man to be in the kingdom is not to reign with Christ, but only to be numbered among Christ's people; what He says then of him that breaks the commandments is, that he shall indeed be reckoned among Christians, yet the least of them.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Grace+Theology+Press&amp;rft.atitle=Final+Destiny%3A+The+Future+Reign+of+the+Servant+Kings&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fgracetheology.org%2Fbookstore%2Ffinal-destiny-the-future-reign-of-the-servant-kings%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFree+grace+theology" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-:1-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-:1_14-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-:1_14-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite id="CITEREFMinistries" class="citation web cs1">Ministries, GraceLife. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.gracelife.org/resources/grr/?id=31">"A History of Free Grace"</a>. <i>www.gracelife.org</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2023-01-10</span></span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=www.gracelife.org&amp;rft.atitle=A+History+of+Free+Grace&amp;rft.aulast=Ministries&amp;rft.aufirst=GraceLife&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.gracelife.org%2Fresources%2Fgrr%2F%3Fid%3D31&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFree+grace+theology" class="Z3988"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-55">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite id="CITEREFHyles" class="citation book cs1">Hyles, Jack. <i>Enemies of Soul Winning</i>. <q>Exactly what do we mean when we say, "Lordship Salvation"? We are talking about the false doctrine that says that in order for a person to be saved, he must make Jesus the Lord of his life. If that doctrine were true, then no one could be saved, because as long as we are limited by these fleshly bodies, we will be unable to make Jesus totally the Lord of our lives.... There are those who say we have to repent of our sins in order to be saved. No, we have to repent only of the thing that makes us unsaved, and that is unbelief. If a person needs to turn from his sins in order to be saved, what sins does he turn from? Does he turn from pride? Does he turn from selfishness? Does he turn from covetousness? The truth is, nobody can turn from all of his sins until he is raptured and he receives a body like the body of the Saviour.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Enemies+of+Soul+Winning&amp;rft.aulast=Hyles&amp;rft.aufirst=Jack&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFree+grace+theology" class="Z3988"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-88"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-88">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite class="citation web cs1"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.chafer.edu/content.cfm?id=295">"Distinctives"</a>. <i>Chafer Theological Seminary</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2023-05-20</span></span>. <q>We hold fast to free grace—the view that God saves mankind by grace alone through faith alone in Jesus Christ alone. No works before, during, or after the moment of initial faith in Christ contribute anything to the free gift of forgiveness and eternal life that one receives through faith in Jesus Christ. The absence of good works during or after the moment of faith subtracts nothing from one's eternal position in Christ. However, good works determine whether one will receive eternal rewards.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Chafer+Theological+Seminary&amp;rft.atitle=Distinctives&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.chafer.edu%2Fcontent.cfm%3Fid%3D295&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFree+grace+theology" class="Z3988"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-107"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-107">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite id="CITEREFLazar2017" class="citation web cs1">Lazar, Shawn (2017-10-18). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://faithalone.org/blog/wrath-and-righteousness/">"Wrath and Righteousness – Grace Evangelical Society"</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2023-07-23</span></span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Wrath+and+Righteousness+%E2%80%93+Grace+Evangelical+Society&amp;rft.date=2017-10-18&amp;rft.aulast=Lazar&amp;rft.aufirst=Shawn&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Ffaithalone.org%2Fblog%2Fwrath-and-righteousness%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFree+grace+theology" class="Z3988"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-108"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-108">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite id="CITEREFDillow2012" class="citation book cs1">Dillow, Joseph (2012). <i>Final Destiny: The Future Reign of the Servant Kings</i>. pp.&#160;990–1030.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Final+Destiny%3A+The+Future+Reign+of+the+Servant+Kings&amp;rft.pages=990-1030&amp;rft.date=2012&amp;rft.aulast=Dillow&amp;rft.aufirst=Joseph&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFree+grace+theology" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-109"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-109">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Reformed_tradition" class="mw-redirect" title="Reformed tradition">Reformed tradition</a>, for instance, sees repentance as "a heartfelt sorrow for sin, a renouncing of it, and a sincere commitment to forsake it and walk in obedience to Christ" (<a href="/wiki/Wayne_Grudem" title="Wayne Grudem">Wayne Grudem</a>, <i>Systematic Theology</i>, p. 713). Defined as such, it is a component of <a href="/wiki/Religious_conversion" title="Religious conversion">conversion</a> and also of <a href="/wiki/Sanctification" class="mw-redirect" title="Sanctification">sanctification</a>, and it is a regularly recurring element throughout the Christian's life. This repentance cannot be present in unbelievers at all (unless perhaps God is in the process of converting them) because only those truly <a href="/wiki/Regeneration_(theology)" title="Regeneration (theology)">regenerated</a> by God can exercise it.</span>
</li>
